<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d78e1c62915e4bbaace4cb887ac5f8f3">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9c08782c-96ec-4cba-8a32-f3eec0504b26'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d29332284" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered composite oxide materials with O3/P2 biphasic crystallographic structure typically demonstrate a combination of high capacities of the O3 phase and high operation voltages of the P2 phase. However, their practical applications are seriously obstructed by difficulties in thermodynamic phase regulation, complicated electrochemical phase transition, and unsatisfactory cycling life. Herein, we propose an efficient structural evolution strategy from biphase to monophase of Na<sub>0.766+<i>x</i></sub>Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>0.33−<i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2</sub> through Li<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;substitution. The role of Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution not only simplifies the unfavorable phase transition by altering the local coordination of transition metal (TM) cations but also stabilizes the cathode–electrolyte&nbsp;interphase to prevent the degradation of TM cations during battery cycling. As a result, the thermodynamically robust O3-Na<sub>0.826</sub>Li<sub>0.06</sub>Ni<sub>0.27</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode delivers a high capacity of 139.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and shows prolonged cycling life at high rates, with capacity retention of 81.6% at 5 C over 500 cycles. This work establishes a solid relationship between the thermodynamic structure evolution and electrochemistry of layered cathode materials, contributing to the development of long-life sodium-ion batteries.</p></div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2565-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2565-sec-0010-title">1 INTRODUCTION</h2>
         
         <p>Due to the ability to satisfy the grid energy-storage demands and the Earth-abundant, low-cost, and environmental-friendly sodium resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with a similar “rocking-chair” storage behavior are considered more feasible alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0001_R_d29332205e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ Sci">1</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0003_R_d29332205e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - InfoMat">3</a></sup></span> However, the practical application of SIBs has been hindered by the unsatisfactory energy densities and structure stabilities of cathode materials. Thus, significant efforts have been devoted. Among the numerous potential cathode materials investigated so far, sodium-layered transition metal (TM) oxides (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, 0 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1) have delivered huge application superiorities owing to their high electrochemical activities, two-dimensional Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels, tunable chemical compositions, low toxicities, facile scale preparation, and successful application of the analogs (LiCoO<sub>2</sub> and Li[Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub><i>y</i></sub>Mn<sub><i>z</i></sub>]O<sub>2</sub>) in LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0004_R_d29332205e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Storage&nbsp;Mater">4</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0007_R_d29332205e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat Commun">7</a></sup></span> According to the local coordination of Na<sup>+</sup> and the number of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers with different oxygen stackings, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> cathodes are mainly categorized into P2-type (prismatic coordination, 0.4 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 0.8) and O3-type (octahedral coordination, 0.8 &lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1), both of which have been extensively studied.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0004_R_d29332205e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Storage&nbsp;Mater">4</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0008_R_d29332205e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J Am Chem Soc">8</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0010_R_d29332205e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv Sci">10</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To the best of our knowledge, P2-type cathodes can present direct and open pathways for fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion with impressive rate capability and cyclic stability.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0010_R_d29332205e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv Sci">10</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0012_R_d29332205e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem Commun">12</a></sup></span> Due to the insufficient sodium content, however, their specific capacities generally tend to be lower than that of the O3-type cathodes. Nonetheless, O3-type cathodes suffer from slower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics and one or more complex phase transitions. This is due to the intrinsic interstitial tetrahedral sites in the crystallographic frameworks, which induce higher energy barriers for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion from one octahedral site to neighbors and Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering rearrangements during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0013_R_d29332205e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Energy Lett">13</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0015_R_d29332205e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J Mater Chem A">15</a></sup></span> Once being charged to high voltage beyond 4.0 V, particularly, the inevitable phase transitions involving the gliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers as Na<sup>+</sup> depleted in Na-O layers can lead to poor voltage cycling and bad degradation of crystal structure.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0016_R_d29332205e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0020_R_d29332205e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J Mater Chem A">20</a></sup></span> Therefore, the O3-type cathodes generally deliver high initial capacities but rapidly fade at high voltages. Moreover, multiple phase transition induces lattice distortion and volume changes, further deteriorating the structure stability and the cathode–electrolyte interphase (CEI). Meanwhile, the component of electrolytes like FEC can be decomposed into HF, which gradually erodes the cathode with unstable CEI and forms TM fluorides, leading to a significant loss of active materials. The continuous CEI accumulation further consumes the electrolyte and increases impedance, hampering the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Therefore, it is crucial to ameliorate the complicated electrochemical phase transition and unstable CEI to consolidate the superiorities of cathodes.</p>
         
         <p>Previous studies have proposed lowering the upper cut-off voltage, but this comes at the expense of sacrificing the reversible capacity.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0014_R_d29332205e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Nano">14</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0018_R_d29332205e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Mater Chem&nbsp;Front">18</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0021_R_d29332205e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ Sci">21</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0022_R_d29332205e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat Commun">22</a></sup></span> Hence, various metal ions have been reported to regulate the phase structure to mitigate the phase transition of O3-type cathodes. Recently, constructing biphasic intergrowth architectures has attracted attention as an effective approach to enhancing their electrochemical performance. Benefiting from the biphasic intergrowth with interlocking P2 and O3 phases by Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution, as reported by Johnson and colleagues, the P2/O3-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Li<sub>0.3</sub>Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode exhibited a higher rate capability with a smoother voltage profile than the single-phase O3-NaNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0023_R_d29332205e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv Energy Mater">23</a></sup></span> Sun and colleagues revealed that the phase transition of P2 → O2 in the P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode&nbsp;occurring in the potential range of 4.0–4.4 V could be effectively eliminated in a novel Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Mg<sup>2+</sup>/Li<sup>+</sup> co-substituted biphasic P2/O3-Na<sub>7/9</sub>Ni<sub>2/9</sub>Mn<sub>4/9</sub>Fe<sub>1/9</sub>Mg<sub>1/9</sub>Li<sub>1/9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode. Therefore, a superhigh initial capacity (170.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C) and an excellent rate capability even at 10 C were achieved.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0024_R_d29332205e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Funct Mater">24</a></sup></span> In our own work, we have established a thermodynamically stable phase diagram based on the rational biphasic tailoring strategy in P2/O3-Na<sub>0.766</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a></sup></span> Although this design achieved optimal distributions with an impressive capacity of 92 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C and a high energy density approaching 514 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, the degradation of the electrode structure still occurred at voltage over 4.0 V due to&nbsp;multiple phase transitions, including P2 → Z of P2 component and P3 → O′3 of O3 component, which definitely induced a serious impact on cycling stability with a low capacity retention of 79.2% after 100 cycles. It should be emphasized that all aforementioned biphasic intergrowth architectures were primarily derived from the substitution of Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions in the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs with other functional metal cations (Li<sup>+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Ti<sup>4+</sup>, etc.). However, the ideal ratios of each phase component in those cathodes need to be identified under high throughput experiments, but the potential mechanisms are extremely complex. Hence, further engineering of crystallographic structures to constrict phase transition based on ion substitutions for single-phase configuration remains crucial and urgent for achieving highly expected O3-type cathodes with satisfactory rate charging and cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by our findings that the monovalent Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution can not only build higher Na content containing P2-type cathodes but also can expand the solid-solution reaction region to suppress the phase transition of P2 → O2, we hypothesized that the Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution could also offer unique contributions to the O3-type cathodes. Starting from the biphasic P2/O3-Na<sub>0.766</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2</sub> compound, therefore, the effects of Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution for Ni<sup>2+</sup> on the crystallographic structure, thermodynamic stability, high-voltage phase transition, Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism, and diffusion kinetics of the resulting materials were investigated in the present study. It was uncovered that when the Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution ratio exceeded 0.04, the P2/O3 biphase of NaLNMFT<i>-x</i> completely transformed into an O3 monophase. Through a combination of time-of-flight secondary-ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) and ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD), multiple microstructural electrochemical characterizations, and theoretical computation, Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution in the thermodynamically stable O3-NaLNMFT monophase demonstrated a dual effect. It not only changes the local coordination of TM cations to simplify the unfavorable high-voltage phase transition of P3–O3′ above 4 V but also stabilizes the CEI to prevent the degradation of TM cations. Additionally, the cathode showed fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, reduced energy barrier,&nbsp;and increased thermodynamic stability. As a result, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance was comprehensively enhanced with a high capacity of 119.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 C and impressive capacity retention of 81.6% after 500 cycles with ignored volume variation. When assembled into a full cell with hard carbon (HC) as the anode, the energy density reached 285 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>, and the reversible capacity reached 255 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 C, maintaining 80.1% after 200 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cey2565-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cey2565-sec-0020-title">2 RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2>
         
         <p>The NaLNMFT-<i>x</i> cathodes were synthesized via a solid-state reaction method, as described in Section <a class="sectionLink scrollableLink" href="#cey2565-sec-0040">4</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>. The collected powder XRD patterns indicated that all as-prepared samples presented highly crystallized features (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>). Rietveld refinements with low <i>R</i><sub><i>wp</i></sub> values (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0001">1A,B</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>) confirmed that all the diffraction peaks detected in the samples of NaNMFT without Li<sup>+</sup> and NaLNMFT-0.02/-0.04 with Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution well assigned to P2 (space group: with P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc) and O3 (space group: with R_3m) phases.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e845" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0025_R_d29332205e845" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a></sup></span> The specific crystallographic parameters summarized in Tables&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S6</a> demonstrated that all Li, Ni, Mn, Fe, and Ti atoms are uniformly located at TM layers, occupying the octahedral 2a and 3b Wyckoff sites. It should be noted that the Li<sup>+</sup> residing in the Na layer in P2 material can be detected based on the nuclear magnetic resonance measurement when the amount of Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution reaches 0.07 per molecule.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0008_R_d29332205e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J Am Chem Soc">8</a></sup></span> With increasing amount of Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution, the NaLNMFT-<i>x</i> samples underwent a transformation from P2 phase-dominated P2/O3 composite (NaNMFT without Li<sup>+</sup>: 41.09% of O3 phase) to O3 phase-dominated P2/O3 composite (NaLNMFT-0.02: 72.32% of O3; NaLNMFT-0.04: 98.2% of O3). When the Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution reaches 0.06 and above, only the O3 phase was formed in the NaLNMFT-0.06/-0.08/-0.1 samples, as shown in the schematic crystal structure viewed along the <i>a</i>-axis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>). Hence, it indicates that Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution can induce the structure evolution of sodium-layered Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> from P2/O3 biphase to O3 monophase.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2565-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b8c7434c-50ed-4967-a2f1-308065a970f6/cey2565-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b8c7434c-50ed-4967-a2f1-308065a970f6/cey2565-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/493c7c82-f52d-41ea-bd49-ebde15638b09/cey2565-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b8c7434c-50ed-4967-a2f1-308065a970f6/cey2565-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2565-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XRD patterns and Rietveld refinements of (A) NaNMFT and (B) NaLNMFT-0.06 samples, (C) cationic potential evolution process with the Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution increasing, (D) HRTEM image, (E) SAED pattern, (F) STEM-HAADF image, (G) ABF images projected along the [001] zone axis, and (H) EDS elemental mapping of NaLNMFT-0.06 sample. ABF, annular-bright field; SAED, selected area electron diffraction.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Based on the “cationic potential” hypothesis, which states that “if the cation potential is lower, the cathode material is easier to form O3 phase,”<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0026_R_d29332205e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Science">26</a></sup></span> the single O3 phase formed in the NaLNMFT-<i>x</i> samples in our case can be definitely attributed to the reduced overall cationic potential by Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution for Ni<sup>2+</sup> and corresponding increased Na<sup>+</sup> amount due to the principle charge balance in the respective samples. From a quantitative perspective, the cation potential (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e03074d0-fb20-4213-90b8-2da9ddc11705/cey2565-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0001" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">Ф</mi>
                           <mtext>cation</mtext>
                        </msub>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${{\rm{\unicode{x00424}}}}_{\text{cation}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) and weighted average ionic potential (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/98b1b80e-b3f2-4f43-920c-7aaed3679f4f/cey2565-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0002" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <msub>
                              <mi>Ф</mi>
                              <mtext>Na</mtext>
                           </msub>
                           <mo stretchy="true">̅</mo>
                        </mover>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $\mathop{{\unicode{x00424}}_{\text{Na}}}\limits^{&amp;#773;}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) were calculated to analyze the evolution process of P2- and O3-type structure with increasing Li<sup>+</sup> substitution (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0001">1C</a>). Since the cation potentials of NaNMFT and NaLNMFT-0.02/-0.04 cathodes were close to the dividing line (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/cff3df1b-46e1-4f88-8b35-eaf41b1b721d/cey2565-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0003" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">Ф</mi>
                              <mtext>Na</mtext>
                           </msub>
                           <mo stretchy="true">̅</mo>
                        </mover>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $\mathop{{{\rm{\unicode{x00424}}}}_{\text{Na}}}\limits^{&amp;#773;}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> = 0.4065 × <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/66799c3f-238a-4c90-91da-75498478bc54/cey2565-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0004" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0004.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">Ф</mi>
                           <mtext>cation</mtext>
                        </msub>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${{\rm{\unicode{x00424}}}}_{\text{cation}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> + 1.572), the P2/O3 biphase structures were easier to be formed. With further increase in Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution, the distance between the cation potential and the dividing line gradually increased, and then the NaLNMFT-0.06/-0.08/-0.1 samples tended to form O3 monophase. Correspondingly, both Na<sup>+</sup> content and electrostatic attraction of adjacent oxygen layers were increased due to the Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution in the TM layers. Moreover, the unit cell parameter <i>c</i>, indicating the layer spacing of both P2 and O3 in all samples, decreases gradually with increasing Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution, while parameter <i>a</i> decreases first and then increases (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>). Notably, the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 sample exhibited the largest <i>c</i> value (16.300(3) Å) among all pure O3 samples, endowing more open space to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) observations revealed that all samples consisted of irregularly shaped particles, with sizes of 3–5 μm for NaNMFT samples and 1–2 μm for NaLNMFT samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>). Combining the XRD results that determined the grain size of the P2/O3-NaNMFT sample to be 113.3 nm and that of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 sample to be 79.9 nm, it can be concluded that Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution inhibits both grain and particle growth. The distinct two sets of lattice fringes with distances of 2.17&nbsp;and 5.41 Å displayed in the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 sample were well assigned to the (104) and (003) crystal planes of O3 phase (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0001">1D</a>). In contrast, although the NaNMFT sample without Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution also shows two sets of lattice fringes, they correspond to the (002) and (003) planes in the P2 and O3 planes, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a></sup></span> This further confirms the transformation from a P2/O3 biphasic composite to a pure O3 phase compound. The selected area electron diffraction pattern of NaLNMFT-0.06 along [12<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/94a0db32-39ac-4c86-9d76-f53c24382ccf/cey2565-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0005" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0005.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <mn>1</mn>
                           <mo>̅</mo>
                        </mover>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $\mathop{1}\limits^{&amp;#773;}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>] zone axis showed an extremely regular array, consistent with the R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/14ae18de-ffba-4901-99ef-a404f98332e6/cey2565-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0006" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0006.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mo>̅</mo>
                        </mover>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $\mathop{3}\limits^{&amp;#773;}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>m space group (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0001">1E</a>). The detailed atomic-scale crystallographic features were further revealed using aberration-corrected scanning transmission electron microscopy&nbsp;(STEM) techniques under high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) and annular-bright field (ABF). Along the [001] zone axis, the TM positions of Ni<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>4+</sup>, Fe<sup>3+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup> are presented by the bright dots in the&nbsp;HAADF-STEM&nbsp;image (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0001">1F</a>) and dark dots in the&nbsp;ABF-STEM image (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0001">1G</a>). The arrangement of TM cations in a hexagonal symmetry matches well with the O3-layered atomic models with R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/067eee70-4fa0-4187-81c0-4df20dfe718d/cey2565-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0007" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0007.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <mover accent="true">
                           <mn>3</mn>
                           <mo>̅</mo>
                        </mover>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> $\mathop{3}\limits^{&amp;#773;}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>m space group as displayed in the inset. The absence of dislocations and defects in the lattice further indicates the high crystallinity. With the exception of the Li<sup>+</sup> being difficult to capture owing to its weak electron beam scattering and lightweight, the energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mappings further confirm that the uniformly distributed elements in NaLNMFT-0.06 sample (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0001">1H</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of all NaLNMFT-<i>x</i> samples was evaluated as cathodes versus a metal Na anode in half-cells within a voltage window of 2.2−4.3 V. The rate capability was first assessed at various rates ranging from 0.1&nbsp;to 10 C (1 C = 210 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2A</a>). As anticipated based on the above microstructural analysis, the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode displayed the best rate capability among all NaLNMFT cathodes dominated by the O3 phase. It delivered high average capacities of 139.4, 131.8, 127.4, 123.2, 116.8,&nbsp;and 103.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 C, respectively. Even tested at 10 C, its capacity with more than 89.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was still 64.7% of that at 0.1 C. In contrast, the retentions of NaLNMFT-0.02/-0.04/-0.08/-0.1 cathodes were only 51.2%, 46.2%, 58.5%, and 60.1%, respectively. Upon returning to 0.2 C, the capacity of O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 recovered&nbsp;to 134.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, nearly achieving 100% recovery. Although the P2/O3-NaNMFT biphasic cathode delivered the highest capacities at low rates, particularly at 0.2 C (142.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and 0.5 C (137.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), the capacity decreased dramatically at higher rates from 1 C. The capacity retention at 10 C was only 63.8%. It indicates that the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode was unstable in terms of crystal structure or Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior at high rates. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2B</a> and&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> display the corresponding galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of the first cycle at each rate for all cathodes. The P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode exhibited a flat plateau above 4.12 V during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction at low rates, which, however,&nbsp;disappeared as the rate increased. Reversely, the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06/-0.08/-0.1 cathodes presented smooth GCD curves. This indicates that the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode underwent a remarkable phase transition while the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode maintained a robust solid-solution reaction with fast Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and unchanged diffusion behavior. However, too much Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution for Ni<sup>2+</sup> will diminish the electrochemical activity of the cathode and limit the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2565-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cad1e930-dc59-4d02-9284-7bbac028824f/cey2565-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cad1e930-dc59-4d02-9284-7bbac028824f/cey2565-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/43cfbd0c-e241-4942-87fa-ca10f1c09011/cey2565-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cad1e930-dc59-4d02-9284-7bbac028824f/cey2565-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2565-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">(A) Rate capability comparison among all the as-prepared samples as cathodes of Na<sup>+</sup> half-cells. (B) GCD profiles of O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode at different rates increased from 0.1&nbsp;to 10 C. Cycling performance comparisons between the P2/O3-NaNMFT and O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathodes at (C) 1 C and (E) 5 C. (D) GCD profiles at 1 C for the first, 50th, 100th, 150th, and 200th cycles. (F, G) XANES spectra of Ni and Fe K-edges of O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode at given states. (H) The capacity and retention comparisons between the NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode and recently reported layered oxides.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Long-term cycling tests were performed at both 1&nbsp;and 5 C to evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage durability of P2/O3-NaNMFT and O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathodes. As revealed in Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2C</a>, the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode delivered a higher initial charge capacity of 142.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 87.3% at 1 C. The O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode showed a relatively lower capacity of 131.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> but achieved an impressive initial CE of 93.75%. Although the CE of both cathodes was as high as 99.5%–100% during the subsequently repeated Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion reactions, the GCD curves of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode remained unchanged in shape compared to those of the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2D</a>). As a result, a capacity of 116.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the retention of 89.6% achieved by O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode was much higher than that of P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode (94.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 65.6%) after 200 cycles. When the rate increased to 5 C, the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode still delivered an impressive initial capacity of 119.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a final reversible capacity of 97.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (81.5% retention) after 500 cycles (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2E</a>). The capacity decay per cycle was only 0.03%. More impressively, this cycling performance is basically the state of the art among all Li<sup>+</sup>-substituted O3-NaLNMFT cathodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and&nbsp;Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7–S9</a>). However, the initial and final charge capacities at 5 C of P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode were only 105 and 79.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) testing was carried out on the Mn element of the O3-NLNMFT-0.06 cathode, and the two major peaks in the spectrum at 653.3&nbsp;and 644.15 eV, well consistent with the previous results of Mn<sup>4+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0016_R_d29332205e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0024_R_d29332205e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Funct Mater">24</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0025_R_d29332205e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0027_R_d29332205e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem">27</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0038_R_d29332205e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Energy Mater">38</a></sup></span> Generally, Mn atoms existing&nbsp;as 4+ in cathode are electrochemically inactive and do not participate in reactions.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0027_R_d29332205e1181" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem">27</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0038_R_d29332205e1181" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Energy Mater">38</a></sup></span> Therefore, the charge compensation upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion of the O3-NLNMFT-0.06 cathode should be attributed to the Ni or Fe atoms, of which the chemical valence and local environment changes were explored by conducting ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) measurements. Remarkably, the X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra of Ni K-edge (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2F</a>) and Fe K-edge (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2G</a>) revealed that the Ni atoms mainly existed in 2+ while Fe atoms were between 2+ and 3+ in the pristine cathode.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0016_R_d29332205e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0024_R_d29332205e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Funct Mater">24</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0025_R_d29332205e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0027_R_d29332205e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem">27</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0038_R_d29332205e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Energy Mater">38</a></sup></span> A significant shift toward&nbsp;higher energy (3.5 eV) of Ni K-edge unraveled a redox reaction from 2+ to 3+ and partially to 4+ with charging to 4.3 V. This shift was higher than the redox change from Ni<sup>2+</sup> to Ni<sup>3+</sup> (∼2 eV) but smaller than that from Ni<sup>2+</sup> to Ni<sup>4+</sup> (∼4 eV).<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0016_R_d29332205e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0024_R_d29332205e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Funct Mater">24</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0025_R_d29332205e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0027_R_d29332205e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem">27</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0038_R_d29332205e1205" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Energy Mater">38</a></sup></span> Moreover, an obvious energy shift toward&nbsp;a higher energy of ~2.0 eV also occurred for the Fe K-edge, suggesting the oxidation of Fe atoms to higher valence states of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup>. Upon discharging the cell to 2.2 V with Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, both the Ni K-edge and Fe K-edge returned to their pristine positions, manifesting that their electrochemical reduction reactions involved in the charge compensation were highly reversible. Under the effect of Li<sup>+</sup>-substitution, therefore, the inhibition of high-voltage phase transition (P3–O3′) and outstanding capacity contribution of Ni and Fe atoms promoted that the electrochemical performance of O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode outperformed lots of previous reported layered oxides (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0002">2H</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0016_R_d29332205e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew Chem Int Ed">16</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0024_R_d29332205e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv Funct Mater">24</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0025_R_d29332205e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0027_R_d29332205e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem">27</a>-<a href="#cey2565-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0038_R_d29332205e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv Energy Mater">38</a></sup></span> To gain additional insights into the practical prospect of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode, full cells were assembled by coupling it with the pre-sodiated HC anode. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> shows the GCD curves for the initial five cycles and the long cycling performance of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06/HC full cell at 1 C within 1.0–4.3 V after activation at 0.1 C for 10 cycles. Impressively, the full cell not only delivered a high initial charge capacity of ~206.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high CE of 90.6% but also the CEs were over 98% with highly overlapped GCD profiles afterward. Even after 200 cycles, it still can deliver 150.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a promising energy density (285 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>) and high capacity retention (80.1%). Such an excellent rate capability and cycling stability certified in both half and full cells indicate that the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode is a promising candidate for SIB application.</p>
         
         <p>The GCD curves and corresponding <i>dQ</i>/<i>dV</i> profiles for the initial four cycles at 0.1 C are displayed to compare the electrochemical reaction behaviors between the P2/O3-NaNMFT and O3-NaLNMTF-0.06 cathodes. Although the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode delivered higher capacities in the initial stage (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3A</a>), as reported in our previous work, the multiple phase transition (P2/O3–P2/O3/P3–P2/P3–P2/Z/O′3–Z/O′3) induced by the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion can be clearly reflected by the five pairs of voltage plateaus/redox reaction peaks (3.2/3.1, 3.44/3.36, 3.62/3.53, 3.84/3.38, and 4.25/4.08 V).<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0009_R_d29332205e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew Chem Int Ed">9</a></sup></span> Those plateaus below 4 V originated from the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering rearrangement, while the one above 4 V was closely associated with phase transitions of P2–Z in the P2 component and P3–O′3 in the O3 component. After the majority of Na<sup>+</sup> was extracted, the residual Na<sup>+</sup> in the host could not stand the repulsion interaction of O–O interlayers, and then the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs glided, leading to deep structural damage. Regarding the cathodes with partial electrochemical active Ni<sup>2+</sup> substituted by Li<sup>+</sup>, the plateaus at above 4 V gradually weakened, and the GCD profiles became much smoother and overlapped with cycling (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3B</a> and&nbsp;Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>). When the Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution reached 0.06 and even more notably, the high voltage plateaus/redox reactions almost disappeared, suggesting that the P3–O′3 phase transitions in those O3-NaLNMTF cathodes were successfully restrained. By contrast, the plateau or redox peak in the low voltage (&lt;4 V) corresponding to the reversible O3–P3 transition gradually became more pronounced.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cey2565-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bd42c426-e34d-4327-9fce-a29d1201c45f/cey2565-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bd42c426-e34d-4327-9fce-a29d1201c45f/cey2565-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/203fef85-4cae-483a-a3bd-aad1be712938/cey2565-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bd42c426-e34d-4327-9fce-a29d1201c45f/cey2565-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cey2565-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcey2.565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The GCD curve and derived <i>dQ</i>/<i>dV</i> profiles for the initial four cycles at 0.1 C of (A) P2/O3-NaNMFT and (B) O3-NaLNMTF-0.06 cathodes. (C, D) Ex situ XRD patterns in given states: pristine, charge to 4.3 V, and discharge to 2.2 V; postmortem analyses on (E) cathode side, (G) anode Na side, and (F) thickness of CEI after 200 cycles at 1 C. (H) TOF-SIMS spectra and (I, J) related three-dimensional bulk distribution collected from the&nbsp;&nbsp;O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode after cycling.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Ex situ XRD patterns were recorded at given states during a cycle to gain more insights into the phase evolutions of all cathodes upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3C,D</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>). Except for the peaks of the Al current collector marked with asterisks, the NaNMFT cathode was confirmed to be a biphasic P2/O3 composite, while the NaLNMFT-0.06 was a single O3 phase again at the initial state. Upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode until 4.3 V, the diffraction peaks of 00l&nbsp;shifted toward&nbsp;higher angles, accompanied by the vanishing of O3 (104) reflection but the appearance of O3′ (003) at 16.6° and P3 peak (105) at 44.8°. This demonstrates that the phase transition from P-type to O-type stacking (P3–O′3) with expanded interlayer spacing is still happening in the O3 component. However, the 00l&nbsp;diffraction peaks shifted to lower angles, and only a new peak (105) appeared for the P3 structure when the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode was charged to 4.3 V, indicating that the high-voltage phase transition (P3–O′3) was restrained indeed.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0015_R_d29332205e1308" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J Mater Chem A">15</a></sup></span> This is in high accordance with the deductions from GCD and <i>dQ</i>/<i>dV</i> profiles. Upon discharging (up to 2.2 V), the diffraction peaks of both cathodes returned to the original positions, indicating that their intrinsic phase components had recovered and the phase transitions were highly reversible. Combined with the electrochemical performance of high capacity at low rates (0.2&nbsp;and 0.5) but poor long-term cycling durability at high current rates (1&nbsp;and 5 C), we hypothesize that the symbiotic interface of P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode could alleviate the structure stress caused by the reversible phase transition of P3–O′3 at low rates through a phase boundary interlocking mechanism but cannot withstand the long-term repeatable structure evolution at high rates. Reversely, a certain Li<sup>+&nbsp;</sup>substitution for Ni<sup>2+</sup> with compatible ionic radii could restrain the high-voltage phase transition of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode and stabilize the crystal structure with improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>Postmortem analyses of the P2/O3-NaNMFT and O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathodes as well as their Na reference electrodes&nbsp;were conducted using SEM and TEM images in the discharge state after 200 cycling tests at 1 C to verify our hypothesis. Clearly, the particle size of the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode became much smaller after high rate charging/discharging (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12A</a>). Conversely, the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode displayed ideal structure integrity with robust active particles that appeared similar in size and morphology to the pristine structure (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3E</a>). Upon observing individual particles, the CEI can be found on the surfaces of both cathodes (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3F</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12B</a>). However, the thickness of CEI on the P2/O3-NaNMFT particle was much thicker and more uneven than that on the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 particle. Furthermore, noticeable holes appeared in the CEI. In our case, this inarguably should be attributed to the severe structure change or degradation caused by the irrepressible phase transition of the cathode. The unstable cathode led the CEI to easily crack and then continuously consumed electrolytes to form a thicker and uneven film. Further, HF formed by the decomposition of electrolyte may corrode the exposed cathode surface and generate TM fluorides, leading to the significant loss of active TM cations and affecting performance. In contrast, the well-distributed, dense, and stable but thin CEI formed on the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 particle not only prevented the continuous loss of electrolyte but also inhibited the dissolution of TM cations into the electrolyte. This is consistent with the results observed in the SEM image. Meanwhile, the thin CEI was beneficial to improving the charge transfer stability and the rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0039_R_d29332205e1337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Small">39</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0040_R_d29332205e1337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Energy Lett">40</a></sup></span> Additionally, as shown in Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3G</a> and&nbsp;Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12B</a>, unlike the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode cells where the Na metal electrode maintained a smooth surface without obvious cracks, the surface of the Na electrode in the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode cell changed significantly to become loose and porous. Undoubtedly, this can be ascribed to the repeated nonuniform deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> during the charge/discharge processes by constantly consuming electrolytes, which led to not only the expansion of metal anodes but also continuous growth of CEI, then reducing the capacity and cycle life of batteries. In some extreme circumstances, the uneven deposition of Na can grow to dendrites and puncture the separator, leading to a short circuit. TOF-SIMS performed on both cycled cathodes and depth profiles (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3H</a> and&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>) demonstrate that their surfaces had various chemical species, including electrolyte components (C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup>, CHO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, and NaCl<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>), metal fluorides (NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, LiF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NiF<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, MnF<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, FeF<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, and TiF<sub><i>x</i></sub><sup>−</sup>), and residual Na species (NaO<sup>–</sup> and NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>). In the P2/O3-NaNMFT cathode, the collected three-dimensional&nbsp;distribution of TM fluorides reveals their presence not only on the surface but also within the bulk of the electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#cey2565-fig-0003">3I,J</a>). The signal probed at a deeper sputtering depth illustrates that a large proportion of TM fluorides is concentrated on the interface. All we know is that the fluorides formed on the surface can lead to cathode degradation, interface impedance increase, and active TM cations loss. Additionally, it hinders the rapid transmission of Na<sup>+</sup> across the interface, further deteriorating the electrochemical performance. Finally, the XRD pattern and cycling performance tests of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode being stored for several months were further performed (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>). Impressively, the cathode displayed an unchanged phase structure and comparable performance, suggesting excellent stability of the designed cathode with promising practicality.</p>
         
         <p>To further investigate the electrochemical reaction kinetics of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode and others, cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were performed with a stepwise scan rate (0.1–1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0004">4A</a>). All curves maintained similar shapes, indicating a further stable electrochemical reaction of the cathode. The linear relationship between peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>), as described by the equation <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, reveals a parameter <i>b</i> value of 0.805, manifesting that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode exhibits a pseudocapacitive feature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>).<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0041_R_d29332205e1427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew Chem Int Ed">41</a></sup></span> To determine the quantitative contribution of capacitive (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i>) reactions, computations were carried out based on equation <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#cey2565-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0041_R_d29332205e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew Chem Int Ed">41</a>, <a href="#cey2565-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cey2565-bib-0042_R_d29332205e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Environ&nbsp;Mater">42</a></sup></span> The results show that the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode presents a super high capacitance contribution, accounting for more than 92% of the total capacity, and this contribution increases with the scan rate (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0004">4B</a>). As a typical example, the capacitive contribution reaches 96% at a scan rate of 0.4 mV s<sup>−1</sup> based on fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (Figure <a href="#cey2565-fig-0004">4C</a>). Meanwhile, the results of CV measurement and corresponding capacitive contribution for other cathodes are also compared and displayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a484bc91-c49c-435a-9975-5c41208094f1/cey2565-math-0059.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0059" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0059.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>D</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <msup>
                                 <mtext>Na</mtext>

                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                        </msub>
                     </mrow>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex"> ${D}_{{\text{Na}}^{+}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) of the O3-NaLNMFT-0.06 cathode was determined by testing the galvanostatic intermittent titration techniques (GITTs) for new cells. The rapid increase of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e5ee22c4-64be-41e7-8ea0-9925cf6a5864/cey2565-math-0061.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:26379368:media:cey2565:cey2565-math-0061" location="graphic/cey2565-math-0061.png">
